# I-carney-002 — AWS Canada Central (ca-central-1) infrastructure as code

GH#470. Critical-path day 13-14. Provisions the Carney production environment in AWS Montréal (ca-central-1) using Terraform: VPC + EC2 m7i-flex.4xlarge + ALB + ACM + Route 53 + SSM Parameter Store + EBS snapshots + S3 audit bucket.

The I-carney-005 docker compose stack (`docker-compose.v6.yml`) is the workload that runs on the EC2 instance. This issue is the cloud substrate that hosts it.

## Files I have ALSO checked clean (§-1.2 #2)

- `docker-compose.v6.yml` — single-host compose with redis+api+worker+webui. Maps host ports 8000 + 3000. State persisted in `shared_state` named volume + bind-mounted `./outputs ./logs ./data`. EC2 instance must have `/var/lib/polaris` directory with the right perms for the bind mounts.
- `Dockerfile.v6` + `web/Dockerfile` — build on the EC2 host directly via `docker compose build`. No need for ECR in v1 (single-host).
- `docs/deploy_runbook.md` — operator runbook assumes single-host. AWS deployment doc lives separately at `infra/aws/README.md` (this PR).
- `polaris-controls/CHARTER.md` — admin authority + cage constraints continue under AWS deployment. SSH from operator workstation via SSM Session Manager (no inbound SSH from internet).
- `state/polaris_restart/plan.md` §7.B LOCKED B1 — auto-merge stays in force on the AWS bring-up branch.
- `outputs/polaris_demo_pubkey.asc` (from I-carney-005 bootstrap) — published at `https://polaris.<domain>/transparency/pubkey.asc` for reviewers (I-carney-003 mounts this).

## Scope

10 files in `infra/aws/` directory + 1 doc file:

1. **`infra/aws/main.tf`** — root Terraform module, AWS provider region=ca-central-1, S3 backend for state (bucket name from variable `tf_state_bucket`).

2. **`infra/aws/vpc.tf`** — uses `terraform-aws-modules/vpc/aws` v5.x:
   - 1 VPC (10.0.0.0/16)
   - 2 public subnets (one per AZ ca-central-1a + ca-central-1b for ALB)
   - 2 private subnets (one per AZ for EC2 instances)
   - 1 NAT gateway (single-AZ for cost; HA-NAT is a Phase-2 follow-up)
   - VPC flow logs to CloudWatch with 30-day retention

3. **`infra/aws/ec2.tf`** — single m7i-flex.4xlarge instance:
   - Ubuntu 24.04 LTS AMI (data source lookup for latest)
   - Instance profile with: SSM Managed Instance Core (for Session Manager) + read access to specific Parameter Store paths under `/polaris/v6/` + read access to specific S3 prefixes
   - Security group: NO inbound 22, NO inbound 80/443 (ALB terminates TLS and forwards on 8000+3000 via private network); outbound 443 to anywhere (pulls Docker images + LLM API egress)
   - 200 GB gp3 root volume + 500 GB gp3 data volume mounted at `/var/lib/polaris` (Docker volumes + bind mounts land here)
   - User-data: install docker compose v2 + clone the polaris repo to `/opt/polaris` + pull secrets from SSM into `/opt/polaris/.env` + `docker compose -f docker-compose.v6.yml up -d` (idempotent on reboot)
   - Daily EBS snapshot via AWS Backup with 7-day retention

4. **`infra/aws/alb.tf`** — Application Load Balancer:
   - Public-facing, dualstack (IPv4+IPv6)
   - HTTPS:443 listener with ACM cert
   - Two target groups: `webui` → instance:3000, `api` → instance:8000
   - Listener rules: `/api/v6/*` → api TG, `/stream/*` → api TG (SSE), everything else → webui TG
   - HTTP:80 → 301 to HTTPS
   - WAF v2 attached with AWS managed rules (CommonRuleSet + KnownBadInputs)
   - Access logs to S3 bucket with 30-day retention

5. **`infra/aws/acm.tf`** — ACM certificate for `polaris.<domain>` + `*.polaris.<domain>` via DNS validation against Route 53 zone (zone is pre-existing, looked up by variable `route53_zone_name`).

6. **`infra/aws/route53.tf`** — A record `polaris.<domain>` → ALB alias.

7. **`infra/aws/ssm_parameters.tf`** — SSM Parameter Store entries (SecureString) for:
   - `/polaris/v6/openrouter_api_key`
   - `/polaris/v6/serper_api_key`
   - `/polaris/v6/semantic_scholar_api_key` (optional)
   - `/polaris/v6/polaris_gpg_key_id`
   - Values supplied via Terraform vars marked sensitive. The EC2 user-data fetches these via `aws ssm get-parameter --with-decryption` and writes them to `/opt/polaris/.env`.

8. **`infra/aws/s3_audit_bucket.tf`** — versioned S3 bucket for daily exported audit bundles. Lifecycle: transition to Glacier Instant Retrieval after 30 days, expire after 1 year. KMS encryption with customer-managed key. Bucket policy denies non-TLS access.

9. **`infra/aws/variables.tf`** + **`infra/aws/outputs.tf`** + **`infra/aws/terraform.tfvars.example`** — variable declarations + outputs (instance_id, alb_dns_name, route53_record) + example tfvars file.

10. **`infra/aws/cloud-init.sh`** — user-data script that the EC2 instance executes on first boot:
    - apt update + install docker.io + docker-compose-plugin + git + awscli
    - mkdir -p /opt/polaris && git clone https://github.com/aldrinor/polaris.git /opt/polaris (specific commit SHA pinned via tfvar)
    - mount data volume at /var/lib/polaris with ext4 + write /etc/fstab entry for reboot persistence
    - fetch SSM parameters → write /opt/polaris/.env
    - run `bash scripts/bootstrap_gpg_demo_key.sh` for the demo key
    - `docker compose -f /opt/polaris/docker-compose.v6.yml up -d`

11. **`infra/aws/README.md`** — operator doc:
    - Prereqs: AWS CLI configured for ca-central-1, pre-existing Route 53 zone, IAM admin
    - Step 1: `terraform init` (S3 backend bootstrap)
    - Step 2: copy `terraform.tfvars.example` to `terraform.tfvars`, fill in secrets
    - Step 3: `terraform plan` then `terraform apply`
    - Step 4: wait ~5 min for EC2 cloud-init; `curl https://polaris.<domain>/api/v6/health`
    - Rollback: `terraform destroy`
    - Cost: ~$280/mo (m7i-flex.4xlarge + ALB + NAT + 700 GB EBS + S3) — for Carney demo timeframe only; tear down after the meeting.

## Acceptance criteria

1. `terraform fmt` exits clean (Terraform 1.6+)
2. `terraform validate` exits clean for ca-central-1 provider
3. NO inbound SSH security group rule — SSM Session Manager is the only access path
4. NO public-facing EC2 — instance is in private subnets, ALB is the only public surface
5. ALB listener routes `/stream/*` to the api target group (SSE pass-through with idle timeout >= 300s)
6. S3 audit bucket has versioning + KMS-CMK encryption + TLS-only bucket policy
7. EBS daily snapshot via AWS Backup with 7-day retention
8. `terraform.tfvars.example` is committed; the real `terraform.tfvars` is gitignored
9. `infra/aws/README.md` has prereqs / step1-4 / rollback / cost sections

## Direct questions iter 1

1. Single-AZ NAT + single-EC2 (no HA) for the Carney demo — APPROVE'd? Or want multi-AZ from day 1?
2. m7i-flex.4xlarge (16 vCPU / 64 GB RAM) as the workload size — APPROVE'd, or want a different instance family (c7i, r7i, m7i-large)?
3. Cloud-init clones the repo from public GitHub at a pinned commit SHA — APPROVE'd? Or want ECR + pre-built images instead to avoid network-during-boot?
4. ALB pass-through for SSE `/stream/*` with `idle_timeout >= 300s` (vs CloudFront which terminates SSE at edge) — APPROVE'd?
5. AWS Backup daily EBS snapshots + 7-day retention vs Lifecycle Manager (DLM) — APPROVE'd?
6. Demo cost ~$280/mo accepted as Carney-demo-window-only (Codex memory note: cost is not a factor in model picks; this is procurement context). APPROVE'd as documented operator cost or want to suppress?
7. Anything else blocking iter-1 APPROVE?

This study sheds light on the complex composition of AFFFs at this particularly contaminated area, emphasizing the necessity for extensive contaminant characterization as sound basis for informed management strategies to mitigate their adverse effects. AFFF PFAS are often named differently in the literature, leading to inconsistency in terminology. To address this issue, we introduced partially new terminology for AFFF-related PFAS to establish consistent terminology, to facilitate communication of identified compounds, and to ensure that the chemical structure can be directly derived from acronyms.\nGraphical Abstract\nSupplementary Information\nThe online version contains supplementary material available at 10.1007/s00216-024-05611-3.\nKeywords: AFFF, PFAS, Terminology, HRMS, Non-target screening, Identification, Soil\nIntroduction\nIn 2021, a new definition for the group of per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances (PFAS) was released by the Organisation for Economic Co-Operation and Development (OECD), which categorizes all substances with a CF2 or CF3 group (excluded CF2H and CF2-halogens) as PFAS, resulting in more than 7 million single substances that fall into the PFAS category [1–3]. Another definition of PFAS by Buck et al. [4] from 2011 includes all compounds with perfluoroalkyl moieties (CnF2n+1), where n denotes the number of carbon atoms. This results in more than 4700 substances [4]. Due to an increasing awareness of PFAS even outside the research community and extensive application combined with human and environmental health concerns, the European Union aspires to a ban of the entire PFAS class [5]. Earlier regulations of PFAS on international level comprise the three popular PFAS representatives, perfluorooctanoic acid (PFOA), perfluorooctane sulfonic acid (PFOS), and perfluorohexane sulfonic acid (PFHxS), into the Stockholm Convention of persistent organic pollutants [6].\nThese regulations were enacted since various PFAS are increasingly associated with adverse human health effects such as thyroid disorder, liver damage, kidney and testicular cancer, and lower birth weight, [7] as well as environmental concerns due to potential bioaccumulation [8–10] and high persistence in the environment [11, 12].\nHowever, due to their unique chemistry, PFAS show attractive properties for industrial applications such as high surface activity, hydro- and oleophobicity, as well as high chemical and thermal stability [13]. Produced since the 1950s, PFAS are now used in nearly every branch of industry and are ingredients in numerous everyday consumer products [14].\nA major field of PFAS application is the use in fluorosurfactant-based firefighting foams such as aqueous film-forming foams (AFFFs), which are used for extinguishing class B fires caused by hydrocarbon-based liquids [15]. PFAS ensure stability of the foam and the formation of a water film between the burning material and the foam, preventing reignition [14]. AFFFs are produced by various manufacturers and formulations differ in composition [16, 17]. In the past, perfluorocarboxylic and sulfonic acids (PFCAs and PFSAs) and their derivatives were often employed in AFFF formulations and PFOS was the substantial component in the AFFF brand Lightwater from 3M, a former major AFFF producer, until 3M phased-out PFOS in the early 2000s [15, 18–20]. 3M ceased AFFF production in 2002 but was known to utilize both perfluorinated and polyfluorinated substances generated by electrochemical fluorination (ECF) over the years, while other manufacturers usually employ polyfluorinated fluorotelomer-based PFAS in their formulations [16, 20, 21]. Common, well-known AFFF PFAS include perfluoroalkyl sulfonamides propyl dimethylamines (PFASAm-Pr-DiMeAms, e.g., in 3M AFFF, Angus), fluorotelomer sulfonamide propyl betaines (FTSAm-Pr-Bs, e.g., in National Foam, Fireade, Angus), and n:1:2 fluorotelomer betaines (n:1:2 FTBs, e.g., in Buckeye, Ansulite) (Electronic Supplementary Material, ESM 2). Due to the phase-out from C8-based PFAS chemistry, the trend in AFFF formulations shifted towards short-chain PFAS [14]. As AFFF formulations do not have to be disclosed by manufacturers, large fractions of PFAS in AFFFs remain unknown [16, 17]. Since AFFFs are directly discharged into the environment during a fire or training event, sites impacted by AFFFs are often heavily contaminated [18, 22, 23].\nNumerous studies showed that various AFFF PFAS can ultimately transform to legacy PFAS such as perfluoroalkyl acids (PFAAs), fluorotelomer sulfonic acids (FTSAs), or fluorotelomer sulfonamides (FTSAms) by photochemical oxidation [24, 25] or biodegradation [25–28]. PFAAs are not further transformed under environmental conditions and can thus accumulate in soil and groundwater over time [29]. While short-chain PFAS are more mobile and can migrate into groundwater, longer chain PFAS are mostly retained in the top layers of soil [29, 30]. This is especially true for zwitterionic and cationic AFFF compounds due to electrostatic interaction with negatively charged soil particles [31, 32]. Thus, for risk assessment and remediation management of AFFF-impacted field sites, a comprehensive characterization of the contaminant pattern is crucial. To accurately characterize the contamination pattern in AFFF-impacted soils, it is essential to select an appropriate extraction method. A simple methanolic extraction, as is often used for soil samples contaminated with anionic PFAS, is insufficient for cationic and zwitterionic AFFF-PFAS [33]. Thus, more exhaustive methods have been developed for soils contaminated with AFFF, employing the combination of acidic and basic extraction solvents [33] or various additives to methanol [34].\nNon-target screening (NTS) by high-resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) is an important tool to identify unknown PFAS in AFFF formulations and impacted field sites, especially since analytical reference standards are often not commercially available which considerably limits target analysis [35]. NTS approaches enabled to identify novel PFAS in AFFF formulations [17, 21, 23, 36, 37] which were then also increasingly detected in groundwater [22, 23, 38], surface water [15], and soils [33, 38, 39] from AFFF-impacted field sites.\nOne of the challenges in NTS is to achieve appropriate data reduction without losing features of interest. Various strategies for PFAS have been described that utilize the intrinsic compound properties [40]. Since PFAS often occur in homologous series with characteristic repeating units of CF2, C2F4, or CF2O moieties, Kendrick-mass defect analysis can be applied [41]. Mass defects of PFAS usually fall into a range from − 0.2 to + 0.1 Da which also offers a filtering possibility. Furthermore, it was shown that the novel MD/C-m/C approach, where for each substance the mass defect per carbon number (MD/C) is plotted against the mass per carbon number (m/C), can reliably separate potential PFAS from many common organic compounds coextracted from the sample matrix [42, 43]. On the MS2 level, diagnostic fragments or recurring fragment mass differences can be utilized for identification and structure elucidation [44].\nThe terminology of PFAS has largely followed the conventions established by Buck et al. [4]. However, typical PFAS in AFFF formulations are anionic, cationic, or zwitterionic and can contain multiple functional groups and novel structures are frequently employed, diverging from those described by Buck et al. [4]. These AFFFs often receive varied, inconsistent acronyms and names in the literature. For instance, the major AFFF component Capstone B/Forafac 1157 (CAS 34455–29-3) is commonly abbreviated as 6:2 FTAB [25], 6:2 FTSaB [22], or CMAmP-6:2 FASA [23]. To address this issue, we propose a consistent and easy identifiable system for acronyms of AFFF PFAS and their transformation products (TPs) based on the terminology of Buck et al. [4].\nIn Reilingen, southwest Germany, a major fire incident occurred in a mattress warehouse in 2008. Various fire departments from the region were involved in firefighting activities, which led to a massive deployment of different AFFF formulations. The AFFFs drained into an irrigation trench across the street and an adjacent agricultural area. An extensive characterization of AFFF PFAS in this area was still pending. The goals of this study were therefore to (i) identify the prevailing PFAS contamination in soil and groundwater by target and non-target screening and to (ii) introduce consistent and identifiable acronyms for AFFF PFAS. This includes by-products and transformation products of AFFF compounds.\nMaterials and methods\nChemicals and reagents\nMethanol (MeOH), ammonium acetate (NH4Ac), and water were of optima LC–MS grade and were purchased",

state/polaris_restart\codex_verdict_iter_2.txt:5467:state\codex_consultations\bootstrap_round2_response.md:470:?? outputs/codex_findings/m42e_code_audit_pass3/
state/polaris_restart\codex_verdict_iter_2.txt:6009:state\codex_consultations\bootstrap_round2_response.md:1305:.\outputs\codex_findings\full_audit_pass_15\codex_stderr.log:3745:outputs\campaigns\CAMP_20260310T073230_fb78bf\WEB_20260310T073230_5da0fc.json:686:      "content": "Abstract\nThe uncontrolled release of aqueous film-forming foam (AFFF) ingredients during a major fire incident in Reilingen, Germany, in 2008 led to significant soil and groundwater contamination. As the identity of fluorochemical surfactants in AFFF are often veiled due to company secrets, it is important to characterize AFFF contaminations and their impact on the environment comprehensively. In this study, we adapted a systematic approach combining a suitable extraction method with liquid chromatography high-resolution quadrupole time-of-flight mass spectrometry (LC-QTOF-MS) for an extensive non-targeted analysis. Our analysis identified 124 per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances (PFAS) from 42 subclasses in the contaminated soil (confidence levels of identification between 1 and 3). Typical for AFFF-impacted field sites, these included anionic, cationic, and zwitterionic substances with perfluoroalkyl chains spanning from 3 to 14 carbon atoms. Furthermore, we identified 1 previously unreported substance, and detected 9 PFAS subclasses for the first time in soil. AFFFs have long been employed to extinguish large hydrocarbon fires, yet their environmental consequences remain a concern. This study sheds light on the complex composition of AFFFs at this particularly contaminated area, emphasizing the necessity for extensive contaminant characterization as sound basis for informed management strategies to mitigate their adverse effects. AFFF PFAS are often named differently in the literature, leading to inconsistency in terminology. To address this issue, we introduced partially new terminology for AFFF-related PFAS to establish consistent terminology, to facilitate communication of identified compounds, and to ensure that the chemical structure can be directly derived from acronyms.\nGraphical Abstract\nSupplementary Information\nThe online version contains supplementary material available at 10.1007/s00216-024-05611-3.\nKeywords: AFFF, PFAS, Terminology, HRMS, Non-target screening, Identification, Soil\nIntroduction\nIn 2021, a new definition for the group of per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances (PFAS) was released by the Organisation for Economic Co-Operation and Development (OECD), which categorizes all substances with a CF2 or CF3 group (excluded CF2H and CF2-halogens) as PFAS, resulting in more than 7 million single substances that fall into the PFAS category [1–3]. Another definition of PFAS by Buck et al. [4] from 2011 includes all compounds with perfluoroalkyl moieties (CnF2n+1), where n denotes the number of carbon atoms. This results in more than 4700 substances [4]. Due to an increasing awareness of PFAS even outside the research community and extensive application combined with human and environmental health concerns, the European Union aspires to a ban of the entire PFAS class [5]. Earlier regulations of PFAS on international level comprise the three popular PFAS representatives, perfluorooctanoic acid (PFOA), perfluorooctane sulfonic acid (PFOS), and perfluorohexane sulfonic acid (PFHxS), into the Stockholm Convention of persistent organic pollutants [6].\nThese regulations were enacted since various PFAS are increasingly associated with adverse human health effects such as thyroid disorder, liver damage, kidney and testicular cancer, and lower birth weight, [7] as well as environmental concerns due to potential bioaccumulation [8–10] and high persistence in the environment [11, 12].\nHowever, due to their unique chemistry, PFAS show attractive properties for industrial applications such as high surface activity, hydro- and oleophobicity, as well as high chemical and thermal stability [13]. Produced since the 1950s, PFAS are now used in nearly every branch of industry and are ingredients in numerous everyday consumer products [14].\nA major field of PFAS application is the use in fluorosurfactant-based firefighting foams such as aqueous film-forming foams (AFFFs), which are used for extinguishing class B fires caused by hydrocarbon-based liquids [15]. PFAS ensure stability of the foam and the formation of a water film between the burning material and the foam, preventing reignition [14]. AFFFs are produced by various manufacturers and formulations differ in composition [16, 17]. In the past, perfluorocarboxylic and sulfonic acids (PFCAs and PFSAs) and their derivatives were often employed in AFFF formulations and PFOS was the substantial component in the AFFF brand Lightwater from 3M, a former major AFFF producer, until 3M phased-out PFOS in the early 2000s [15, 18–20]. 3M ceased AFFF production in 2002 but was known to utilize both perfluorinated and polyfluorinated substances generated by electrochemical fluorination (ECF) over the years, while other manufacturers usually employ polyfluorinated fluorotelomer-based PFAS in their formulations [16, 20, 21]. Common, well-known AFFF PFAS include perfluoroalkyl sulfonamides propyl dimethylamines (PFASAm-Pr-DiMeAms, e.g., in 3M AFFF, Angus), fluorotelomer sulfonamide propyl betaines (FTSAm-Pr-Bs, e.g., in National Foam, Fireade, Angus), and n:1:2 fluorotelomer betaines (n:1:2 FTBs, e.g., in Buckeye, Ansulite) (Electronic Supplementary Material, ESM 2). Due to the phase-out from C8-based PFAS chemistry, the trend in AFFF formulations shifted towards short-chain PFAS [14]. As AFFF formulations do not have to be disclosed by manufacturers, large fractions of PFAS in AFFFs remain unknown [16, 17]. Since AFFFs are directly discharged into the environment during a fire or training event, sites impacted by AFFFs are often heavily contaminated [18, 22, 23].\nNumerous studies showed that various AFFF PFAS can ultimately transform to legacy PFAS such as perfluoroalkyl acids (PFAAs), fluorotelomer sulfonic acids (FTSAs), or fluorotelomer sulfonamides (FTSAms) by photochemical oxidation [24, 25] or biodegradation [25–28]. PFAAs are not further transformed under environmental conditions and can thus accumulate in soil and groundwater over time [29]. While short-chain PFAS are more mobile and can migrate into groundwater, longer chain PFAS are mostly retained in the top layers of soil [29, 30]. This is especially true for zwitterionic and cationic AFFF compounds due to electrostatic interaction with negatively charged soil particles [31, 32]. Thus, for risk assessment and remediation management of AFFF-impacted field sites, a comprehensive characterization of the contaminant pattern is crucial. To accurately characterize the contamination pattern in AFFF-impacted soils, it is essential to select an appropriate extraction method. A simple methanolic extraction, as is often used for soil samples contaminated with anionic PFAS, is insufficient for cationic and zwitterionic AFFF-PFAS [33]. Thus, more exhaustive methods have been developed for soils contaminated with AFFF, employing the combination of acidic and basic extraction solvents [33] or various additives to methanol [34].\nNon-target screening (NTS) by high-resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) is an important tool to identify unknown PFAS in AFFF formulations and impacted field sites, especially since analytical reference standards are often not commercially available which considerably limits target analysis [35]. NTS approaches enabled to identify novel PFAS in AFFF formulations [17, 21, 23, 36, 37] which were then also increasingly detected in groundwater [22, 23, 38], surface water [15], and soils [33, 38, 39] from AFFF-impacted field sites.\nOne of the challenges in NTS is to achieve appropriate data reduction without losing features of interest. Various strategies for PFAS have been described that utilize the intrinsic compound properties [40]. Since PFAS often occur in homologous series with characteristic repeating units of CF2, C2F4, or CF2O moieties, Kendrick-mass defect analysis can be applied [41]. Mass defects of PFAS usually fall into a range from − 0.2 to + 0.1 Da which also offers a filtering possibility. Furthermore, it was shown that the novel MD/C-m/C approach, where for each substance the mass defect per carbon number (MD/C) is plotted against the mass per carbon number (m/C), can reliably separate potential PFAS from many common organic compounds coextracted from the sample matrix [42, 43]. On the MS2 level, diagnostic fragments or recurring fragment mass differences can be utilized for identification and structure elucidation [44].\nThe terminology of PFAS has largely followed the conventions established by Buck et al. [4]. However, typical PFAS in AFFF formulations are anionic, cationic, or zwitterionic and can contain multiple functional groups and novel structures are frequently employed, diverging from those described by Buck et al. [4]. These AFFFs often receive varied, inconsistent acronyms and names in the literature. For instance, the major AFFF component Capstone B/Forafac 1157 (CAS 34455–29-3) is commonly abbreviated as 6:2 FTAB [25], 6:2 FTSaB [22], or CMAmP-6:2 FASA [23]. To address this issue, we propose a consistent and easy identifiable system for acronyms of AFFF PFAS and their transformation products (TPs) based on the terminology of Buck et al. [4].\nIn Reilingen, southwest Germany, a major fire incident occurred in a mattress warehouse in 2008. Various fire departments from the region were involved in firefighting activities, which led to a massive deployment of different AFFF formulations. The AFFFs drained into an irrigation trench across the street and an adjacent agricultural area. An extensive characterization of AFFF PFAS in this area was still pending. The goals of this study were therefore to (i) identify the prevailing PFAS contamination in soil and groundwater by target and non-target screening and to (ii) introduce consistent and identifiable acronyms for AFFF PFAS. This includes by-products and transformation products of AFFF compounds.\nMaterials and methods\nChemicals and reagents\nMethanol (MeOH), ammonium acetate (NH4Ac), and water were of optima LC–MS grade and were purchased"

PFAS ensure stability of the foam and the formation of a water film between the burning material and the foam, preventing reignition [14]. AFFFs are produced by various manufacturers and formulations differ in composition [16, 17]. In the past, perfluorocarboxylic and sulfonic acids (PFCAs and PFSAs) and their derivatives were often employed in AFFF formulations and PFOS was the substantial component in the AFFF brand Lightwater from 3M, a former major AFFF producer, until 3M phased-out PFOS in the early 2000s [15, 18–20]. 3M ceased AFFF production in 2002 but was known to utilize both perfluorinated and polyfluorinated substances generated by electrochemical fluorination (ECF) over the years, while other manufacturers usually employ polyfluorinated fluorotelomer-based PFAS in their formulations [16, 20, 21]. Common, well-known AFFF PFAS include perfluoroalkyl sulfonamides propyl dimethylamines (PFASAm-Pr-DiMeAms, e.g., in 3M AFFF, Angus), fluorotelomer sulfonamide propyl betaines (FTSAm-Pr-Bs, e.g., in National Foam, Fireade, Angus), and n:1:2 fluorotelomer betaines (n:1:2 FTBs, e.g., in Buckeye, Ansulite) (Electronic Supplementary Material, ESM 2). Due to the phase-out from C8-based PFAS chemistry, the trend in AFFF formulations shifted towards short-chain PFAS [14]. As AFFF formulations do not have to be disclosed by manufacturers, large fractions of PFAS in AFFFs remain unknown [16, 17]. Since AFFFs are directly discharged into the environment during a fire or training event, sites impacted by AFFFs are often heavily contaminated [18, 22, 23].\nNumerous studies showed that various AFFF PFAS can ultimately transform to legacy PFAS such as perfluoroalkyl acids (PFAAs), fluorotelomer sulfonic acids (FTSAs), or fluorotelomer sulfonamides (FTSAms) by photochemical oxidation [24, 25] or biodegradation [25–28]. PFAAs are not further transformed under environmental conditions and can thus accumulate in soil and groundwater over time [29]. While short-chain PFAS are more mobile and can migrate into groundwater, longer chain PFAS are mostly retained in the top layers of soil [29, 30]. This is especially true for zwitterionic and cationic AFFF compounds due to electrostatic interaction with negatively charged soil particles [31, 32]. Thus, for risk assessment and remediation management of AFFF-impacted field sites, a comprehensive characterization of the contaminant pattern is crucial. To accurately characterize the contamination pattern in AFFF-impacted soils, it is essential to select an appropriate extraction method. A simple methanolic extraction, as is often used for soil samples contaminated with anionic PFAS, is insufficient for cationic and zwitterionic AFFF-PFAS [33]. Thus, more exhaustive methods have been developed for soils contaminated with AFFF, employing the combination of acidic and basic extraction solvents [33] or various additives to methanol [34].\nNon-target screening (NTS) by high-resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) is an important tool to identify unknown PFAS in AFFF formulations and impacted field sites, especially since analytical reference standards are often not commercially available which considerably limits target analysis [35]. NTS approaches enabled to identify novel PFAS in AFFF formulations [17, 21, 23, 36, 37] which were then also increasingly detected in groundwater [22, 23, 38], surface water [15], and soils [33, 38, 39] from AFFF-impacted field sites.\nOne of the challenges in NTS is to achieve appropriate data reduction without losing features of interest. Various strategies for PFAS have been described that utilize the intrinsic compound properties [40]. Since PFAS often occur in homologous series with characteristic repeating units of CF2, C2F4, or CF2O moieties, Kendrick-mass defect analysis can be applied [41]. Mass defects of PFAS usually fall into a range from − 0.2 to + 0.1 Da which also offers a filtering possibility. Furthermore, it was shown that the novel MD/C-m/C approach, where for each substance the mass defect per carbon number (MD/C) is plotted against the mass per carbon number (m/C), can reliably separate potential PFAS from many common organic compounds coextracted from the sample matrix [42, 43]. On the MS2 level, diagnostic fragments or recurring fragment mass differences can be utilized for identification and structure elucidation [44].\nThe terminology of PFAS has largely followed the conventions established by Buck et al. [4]. However, typical PFAS in AFFF formulations are anionic, cationic, or zwitterionic and can contain multiple functional groups and novel structures are frequently employed, diverging from those described by Buck et al. [4]. These AFFFs often receive varied, inconsistent acronyms and names in the literature. For instance, the major AFFF component Capstone B/Forafac 1157 (CAS 34455–29-3) is commonly abbreviated as 6:2 FTAB [25], 6:2 FTSaB [22], or CMAmP-6:2 FASA [23]. To address this issue, we propose a consistent and easy identifiable system for acronyms of AFFF PFAS and their transformation products (TPs) based on the terminology of Buck et al. [4].\nIn Reilingen, southwest Germany, a major fire incident occurred in a mattress warehouse in 2008. Various fire departments from the region were involved in firefighting activities, which led to a massive deployment of different AFFF formulations. The AFFFs drained into an irrigation trench across the street and an adjacent agricultural area. An extensive characterization of AFFF PFAS in this area was still pending. The goals of this study were therefore to (i) identify the prevailing PFAS contamination in soil and groundwater by target and non-target screening and to (ii) introduce consistent and identifiable acronyms for AFFF PFAS. This includes by-products and transformation products of AFFF compounds.\nMaterials and methods\nChemicals and reagents\nMethanol (MeOH), ammonium acetate (NH4Ac), and water were of optima LC–MS grade and were purchased",

state/polaris_restart\codex_verdict_iter_2.txt:6093:state\codex_consultations\bootstrap_round2_response.md:1412:.\docs\carney_delivery_plan_v6_2.md:373:**Codex Red-Team Checklist** (`.codex/codex_red_team_checklist.md`) is the structured-manifest input Codex uses for step 5. state/polaris_restart\codex_verdict_iter_2.txt:6094:state\codex_consultations\bootstrap_round2_response.md:1413:.\docs\carney_delivery_plan_v5_1_redline.md:80:- **Codex Red-Team Checklist (NEW, fixed independent of Claude's task brief)** at `.codex/codex_red_team_checklist.md`:
state/polaris_restart\codex_verdict_iter_2.txt:6095:state\codex_consultations\bootstrap_round2_response.md:1417:.\docs\carney_delivery_plan_v5_1_redline.md:298:Codex Red-Team Checklist (`.codex/codex_red_team_checklist.md`) is created as a separate file BEFORE Phase 0 starts (not promised in plan text). state/polaris_restart\codex_verdict_iter_2.txt:6117:state\codex_consultations\bootstrap_round2_response.md:1447:.\outputs\codex_findings\m17_review\codex_stdout.log:5821:outputs\campaigns\CAMP_20260310T073230_fb78bf\WEB_20260310T073230_5da0fc.json:686:      "content": "Abstract\nThe uncontrolled release of aqueous film-forming foam (AFFF) ingredients during a major fire incident in Reilingen, Germany, in 2008 led to significant soil and groundwater contamination. As the identity of fluorochemical surfactants in AFFF are often veiled due to company secrets, it is important to characterize AFFF contaminations and their impact on the environment comprehensively. In this study, we adapted a systematic approach combining a suitable extraction method with liquid chromatography high-resolution quadrupole time-of-flight mass spectrometry (LC-QTOF-MS) for an extensive non-targeted analysis. Our analysis identified 124 per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances (PFAS) from 42 subclasses in the contaminated soil (confidence levels of identification between 1 and 3). Typical for AFFF-impacted field sites, these included anionic, cationic, and zwitterionic substances with perfluoroalkyl chains spanning from 3 to 14 carbon atoms. Furthermore, we identified 1 previously unreported substance, and detected 9 PFAS subclasses for the first time in soil. AFFFs have long been employed to extinguish large hydrocarbon fires, yet their environmental consequences remain a concern. This study sheds light on the complex composition of AFFFs at this particularly contaminated area, emphasizing the necessity for extensive contaminant characterization as sound basis for informed management strategies to mitigate their adverse effects. AFFF PFAS are often named differently in the literature, leading to inconsistency in terminology. To address this issue, we introduced partially new terminology for AFFF-related PFAS to establish consistent terminology, to facilitate communication of identified compounds, and to ensure that the chemical structure can be directly derived from acronyms.\nGraphical Abstract\nSupplementary Information\nThe online version contains supplementary material available at 10.1007/s00216-024-05611-3.\nKeywords: AFFF, PFAS, Terminology, HRMS, Non-target screening, Identification, Soil\nIntroduction\nIn 2021, a new definition for the group of per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances (PFAS) was released by the Organisation for Economic Co-Operation and Development (OECD), which categorizes all substances with a CF2 or CF3 group (excluded CF2H and CF2-halogens) as PFAS, resulting in more than 7 million single substances that fall into the PFAS category [1–3]. Another definition of PFAS by Buck et al. [4] from 2011 includes all compounds with perfluoroalkyl moieties (CnF2n+1), where n denotes the number of carbon atoms. This results in more than 4700 substances [4]. Due to an increasing awareness of PFAS even outside the research community and extensive application combined with human and environmental health concerns, the European Union aspires to a ban of the entire PFAS class [5]. Earlier regulations of PFAS on international level comprise the three popular PFAS representatives, perfluorooctanoic acid (PFOA), perfluorooctane sulfonic acid (PFOS), and perfluorohexane sulfonic acid (PFHxS), into the Stockholm Convention of persistent organic pollutants [6].\nThese regulations were enacted since various PFAS are increasingly associated with adverse human health effects such as thyroid disorder, liver damage, kidney and testicular cancer, and lower birth weight, [7] as well as environmental concerns due to potential bioaccumulation [8–10] and high persistence in the environment [11, 12].\nHowever, due to their unique chemistry, PFAS show attractive properties for industrial applications such as high surface activity, hydro- and oleophobicity, as well as high chemical and thermal stability [13]. Produced since the 1950s, PFAS are now used in nearly every branch of industry and are ingredients in numerous everyday consumer products [14].\nA major field of PFAS application is the use in fluorosurfactant-based firefighting foams such as aqueous film-forming foams (AFFFs), which are used for extinguishing class B fires caused by hydrocarbon-based liquids [15]. PFAS ensure stability of the foam and the formation of a water film between the burning material and the foam, preventing reignition [14]. AFFFs are produced by various manufacturers and formulations differ in composition [16, 17]. In the past, perfluorocarboxylic and sulfonic acids (PFCAs and PFSAs) and their derivatives were often employed in AFFF formulations and PFOS was the substantial component in the AFFF brand Lightwater from 3M, a former major AFFF producer, until 3M phased-out PFOS in the early 2000s [15, 18–20]. 3M ceased AFFF production in 2002 but was known to utilize both perfluorinated and polyfluorinated substances generated by electrochemical fluorination (ECF) over the years, while other manufacturers usually employ polyfluorinated fluorotelomer-based PFAS in their formulations [16, 20, 21]. Common, well-known AFFF PFAS include perfluoroalkyl sulfonamides propyl dimethylamines (PFASAm-Pr-DiMeAms, e.g., in 3M AFFF, Angus), fluorotelomer sulfonamide propyl betaines (FTSAm-Pr-Bs, e.g., in National Foam, Fireade, Angus), and n:1:2 fluorotelomer betaines (n:1:2 FTBs, e.g., in Buckeye, Ansulite) (Electronic Supplementary Material, ESM 2). Due to the phase-out from C8-based PFAS chemistry, the trend in AFFF formulations shifted towards short-chain PFAS [14]. As AFFF formulations do not have to be disclosed by manufacturers, large fractions of PFAS in AFFFs remain unknown [16, 17]. Since AFFFs are directly discharged into the environment during a fire or training event, sites impacted by AFFFs are often heavily contaminated [18, 22, 23].\nNumerous studies showed that various AFFF PFAS can ultimately transform to legacy PFAS such as perfluoroalkyl acids (PFAAs), fluorotelomer sulfonic acids (FTSAs), or fluorotelomer sulfonamides (FTSAms) by photochemical oxidation [24, 25] or biodegradation [25–28]. PFAAs are not further transformed under environmental conditions and can thus accumulate in soil and groundwater over time [29]. While short-chain PFAS are more mobile and can migrate into groundwater, longer chain PFAS are mostly retained in the top layers of soil [29, 30]. This is especially true for zwitterionic and cationic AFFF compounds due to electrostatic interaction with negatively charged soil particles [31, 32]. Thus, for risk assessment and remediation management of AFFF-impacted field sites, a comprehensive characterization of the contaminant pattern is crucial. To accurately characterize the contamination pattern in AFFF-impacted soils, it is essential to select an appropriate extraction method. A simple methanolic extraction, as is often used for soil samples contaminated with anionic PFAS, is insufficient for cationic and zwitterionic AFFF-PFAS [33]. Thus, more exhaustive methods have been developed for soils contaminated with AFFF, employing the combination of acidic and basic extraction solvents [33] or various additives to methanol [34].\nNon-target screening (NTS) by high-resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) is an important tool to identify unknown PFAS in AFFF formulations and impacted field sites, especially since analytical reference standards are often not commercially available which considerably limits target analysis [35]. NTS approaches enabled to identify novel PFAS in AFFF formulations [17, 21, 23, 36, 37] which were then also increasingly detected in groundwater [22, 23, 38], surface water [15], and soils [33, 38, 39] from AFFF-impacted field sites.\nOne of the challenges in NTS is to achieve appropriate data reduction without losing features of interest. Various strategies for PFAS have been described that utilize the intrinsic compound properties [40]. Since PFAS often occur in homologous series with characteristic repeating units of CF2, C2F4, or CF2O moieties, Kendrick-mass defect analysis can be applied [41]. Mass defects of PFAS usually fall into a range from − 0.2 to + 0.1 Da which also offers a filtering possibility. Furthermore, it was shown that the novel MD/C-m/C approach, where for each substance the mass defect per carbon number (MD/C) is plotted against the mass per carbon number (m/C), can reliably separate potential PFAS from many common organic compounds coextracted from the sample matrix [42, 43]. On the MS2 level, diagnostic fragments or recurring fragment mass differences can be utilized for identification and structure elucidation [44].\nThe terminology of PFAS has largely followed the conventions established by Buck et al. [4]. However, typical PFAS in AFFF formulations are anionic, cationic, or zwitterionic and can contain multiple functional groups and novel structures are frequently employed, diverging from those described by Buck et al. [4]. These AFFFs often receive varied, inconsistent acronyms and names in the literature. For instance, the major AFFF component Capstone B/Forafac 1157 (CAS 34455–29-3) is commonly abbreviated as 6:2 FTAB [25], 6:2 FTSaB [22], or CMAmP-6:2 FASA [23]. To address this issue, we propose a consistent and easy identifiable system for acronyms of AFFF PFAS and their transformation products (TPs) based on the terminology of Buck et al. [4].\nIn Reilingen, southwest Germany, a major fire incident occurred in a mattress warehouse in 2008. Various fire departments from the region were involved in firefighting activities, which led to a massive deployment of different AFFF formulations. The AFFFs drained into an irrigation trench across the street and an adjacent agricultural area. An extensive characterization of AFFF PFAS in this area was still pending. The goals of this study were therefore to (i) identify the prevailing PFAS contamination in soil and groundwater by target and non-target screening and to (ii) introduce consistent and identifiable acronyms for AFFF PFAS. This includes by-products and transformation products of AFFF compounds.\nMaterials and methods\nChemicals and reagents\nMethanol (MeOH), ammonium acetate (NH4Ac), and water were of optima LC–MS grade and were purchased"

AFFFs are produced by various manufacturers and formulations differ in composition [16, 17]. In the past, perfluorocarboxylic and sulfonic acids (PFCAs and PFSAs) and their derivatives were often employed in AFFF formulations and PFOS was the substantial component in the AFFF brand Lightwater from 3M, a former major AFFF producer, until 3M phased-out PFOS in the early 2000s [15, 18–20]. 3M ceased AFFF production in 2002 but was known to utilize both perfluorinated and polyfluorinated substances generated by electrochemical fluorination (ECF) over the years, while other manufacturers usually employ polyfluorinated fluorotelomer-based PFAS in their formulations [16, 20, 21]. Common, well-known AFFF PFAS include perfluoroalkyl sulfonamides propyl dimethylamines (PFASAm-Pr-DiMeAms, e.g., in 3M AFFF, Angus), fluorotelomer sulfonamide propyl betaines (FTSAm-Pr-Bs, e.g., in National Foam, Fireade, Angus), and n:1:2 fluorotelomer betaines (n:1:2 FTBs, e.g., in Buckeye, Ansulite) (Electronic Supplementary Material, ESM 2). Due to the phase-out from C8-based PFAS chemistry, the trend in AFFF formulations shifted towards short-chain PFAS [14]. As AFFF formulations do not have to be disclosed by manufacturers, large fractions of PFAS in AFFFs remain unknown [16, 17]. Since AFFFs are directly discharged into the environment during a fire or training event, sites impacted by AFFFs are often heavily contaminated [18, 22, 23].\nNumerous studies showed that various AFFF PFAS can ultimately transform to legacy PFAS such as perfluoroalkyl acids (PFAAs), fluorotelomer sulfonic acids (FTSAs), or fluorotelomer sulfonamides (FTSAms) by photochemical oxidation [24, 25] or biodegradation [25–28]. PFAAs are not further transformed under environmental conditions and can thus accumulate in soil and groundwater over time [29]. While short-chain PFAS are more mobile and can migrate into groundwater, longer chain PFAS are mostly retained in the top layers of soil [29, 30]. This is especially true for zwitterionic and cationic AFFF compounds due to electrostatic interaction with negatively charged soil particles [31, 32]. Thus, for risk assessment and remediation management of AFFF-impacted field sites, a comprehensive characterization of the contaminant pattern is crucial. To accurately characterize the contamination pattern in AFFF-impacted soils, it is essential to select an appropriate extraction method. A simple methanolic extraction, as is often used for soil samples contaminated with anionic PFAS, is insufficient for cationic and zwitterionic AFFF-PFAS [33]. Thus, more exhaustive methods have been developed for soils contaminated with AFFF, employing the combination of acidic and basic extraction solvents [33] or various additives to methanol [34].\nNon-target screening (NTS) by high-resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) is an important tool to identify unknown PFAS in AFFF formulations and impacted field sites, especially since analytical reference standards are often not commercially available which considerably limits target analysis [35]. NTS approaches enabled to identify novel PFAS in AFFF formulations [17, 21, 23, 36, 37] which were then also increasingly detected in groundwater [22, 23, 38], surface water [15], and soils [33, 38, 39] from AFFF-impacted field sites.\nOne of the challenges in NTS is to achieve appropriate data reduction without losing features of interest. Various strategies for PFAS have been described that utilize the intrinsic compound properties [40]. Since PFAS often occur in homologous series with characteristic repeating units of CF2, C2F4, or CF2O moieties, Kendrick-mass defect analysis can be applied [41]. Mass defects of PFAS usually fall into a range from − 0.2 to + 0.1 Da which also offers a filtering possibility. Furthermore, it was shown that the novel MD/C-m/C approach, where for each substance the mass defect per carbon number (MD/C) is plotted against the mass per carbon number (m/C), can reliably separate potential PFAS from many common organic compounds coextracted from the sample matrix [42, 43]. On the MS2 level, diagnostic fragments or recurring fragment mass differences can be utilized for identification and structure elucidation [44].\nThe terminology of PFAS has largely followed the conventions established by Buck et al. [4]. However, typical PFAS in AFFF formulations are anionic, cationic, or zwitterionic and can contain multiple functional groups and novel structures are frequently employed, diverging from those described by Buck et al. [4]. These AFFFs often receive varied, inconsistent acronyms and names in the literature. For instance, the major AFFF component Capstone B/Forafac 1157 (CAS 34455–29-3) is commonly abbreviated as 6:2 FTAB [25], 6:2 FTSaB [22], or CMAmP-6:2 FASA [23]. To address this issue, we propose a consistent and easy identifiable system for acronyms of AFFF PFAS and their transformation products (TPs) based on the terminology of Buck et al. [4].\nIn Reilingen, southwest Germany, a major fire incident occurred in a mattress warehouse in 2008. Various fire departments from the region were involved in firefighting activities, which led to a massive deployment of different AFFF formulations. The AFFFs drained into an irrigation trench across the street and an adjacent agricultural area. An extensive characterization of AFFF PFAS in this area was still pending. The goals of this study were therefore to (i) identify the prevailing PFAS contamination in soil and groundwater by target and non-target screening and to (ii) introduce consistent and identifiable acronyms for AFFF PFAS. This includes by-products and transformation products of AFFF compounds.\nMaterials and methods\nChemicals and reagents\nMethanol (MeOH), ammonium acetate (NH4Ac), and water were of optima LC–MS grade and were purchased"

outputs/codex_findings\v27_deep_content_audit\_codex_stdout.txt:1747:outputs\polaris_graph\PG_LB_SA_02.json:2277:      "content": "Sci-Hub: статья отсутствует в базе. Что делать? Вот незадача! Полного текста этой статьи нет в моей базе: Cardiometabolic risk factors efficacy of semaglutide in the STEP program Postgraduate Medicine, 2022 People with overweight or obesity often suffer from associated cardiometabolic diseases and comorbidities. Current therapies for obesity include lifestyle intervention, bariatric surgery, and pharmacotherapy. The magnitude of weight loss achieved with these therapies can determine the level of improvement in various comorbidities. Once-weekly subcutaneous semaglutide 2.4 mg is a glucagon-like peptide-1 receptor agonist recently approved by the US Food and Drug Administration for the treatment of obesity.... 10.1080/00325481.2022.2147325 &rarr; Статья относительно новая - она была опубликована после 2021 года. Такие статьи, в большинстве своем, отсутствуют в базе Sci-Hub по причине сложности их автоматического скачивания в большом количестве. О! Согласно индексу OpenAlex, данная статья находится в открытом доступе. Попробовать скачать её можно тут . Но это не гарантированно, так как индекс часто содержит ошибки. Что делать? Как правило, если статья не какая-то редкая, то её скачают по Вашему запросу в течение нескольких минут. ... после того, как запрос будет решен, статья появится на Sci-Hub и будет всегда доступна бесплатно для любого человека. outputs/codex_findings\v27_deep_content_audit\_codex_stdout.txt:3078:outputs\polaris_graph\baseline_v2\PG_LB_FS_01_health.json:315:      "content": "Landmark Regulatory Approvals in 2025\nSemaglutide (Ozempic) — Kidney Disease Indication\nOn January 28, 2025, the U.S. Food and Drug Administration (FDA) expanded the label of semaglutide (Ozempic®) to include a chronic kidney disease (CKD) protection indication in adults with type 2 diabetes mellitus (T2DM) and CKD (U.S. Food and Drug Administration [FDA], 2025a). This represented the first approval of a GLP-1 receptor agonist for renal outcomes, establishing a new therapeutic benchmark.The approval was based on the pivotal FLOW trial (NCT03819153), a randomized, double-blind, placebo-controlled outcomes study enrolling 3,533 participants across 28 countries. Results showed a 24% reduction in the composite primary endpoint of sustained ≥ 50% eGFR decline, end-stage kidney disease, renal death, or cardiovascular death (HR 0.76; 95% CI, 0.66–0.88; p = 0.0003) (Perkovic & Tuttle, 2024). Secondary analyses confirmed consistent benefits across major outcomes: kidney-specific composite (HR 0.79; 95% CI, 0.66–0.94), cardiovascular death (HR 0.71; 95% CI, 0.56–0.89), major adverse cardiovascular events (HR 0.82; 95% CI, 0.68–0.98), and all-cause mortality (HR 0.80; 95% CI, 0.67–0.95). The mean annual eGFR decline was attenuated by 1.16 mL/min/1.73m² in semaglutide recipients compared with placebo (FLOW Trial Investigators, 2024). These findings are summarized graphically in Fig. 2, which demonstrates hazard ratios and confidence intervals for the key endpoints.19,20\nForest plot showing hazard ratios (95% CI) for the composite kidney + cardiovascular endpoint and major secondary outcomes from the FLOW trial (Perkovic & Tuttle, 2024; FDA, 2025a).\nThe regulatory impact was global. In December 2024, the European Medicines Agency (EMA)’s Committee for Medicinal Products for Human Use (CHMP) adopted a positive opinion for semaglutide’s kidney indication (European Medicines Agency [EMA], 2024). Subsequently, the Therapeutic Goods Administration (TGA) of Australia approved the indication in August 2025 (Therapeutic Goods Administration [TGA], 2025). Collectively, these decisions illustrate growing harmonization of regulatory approaches in addressing the multi-organ burden of diabetes. The clinical implications are profound: semaglutide now provides validated renal and cardiovascular protection in addition to glycemic and weight benefits.21–24 This expands treatment beyond glucose control to a comprehensive cardio-renal-metabolic approach, shifting practice guidelines across endocrinology, nephrology, and cardiology (American Diabetes Association [ADA], 2025). Phase 3 vs dulaglutide (T2D)\n|\nWeight loss and HbA1c superiority vs placebo / dulaglutide (per trials)\n|\nAutoinjector; various mg strengths\n|\nWorld’s first approved dual GLP-1/glucagon agonist\n|\nTable 1 provides a consolidated overview of novel antidiabetic drug approvals during the year 2025, across multiple therapeutic classes and global regulatory regions. Abbreviations: FDA = U.S. Food and Drug Administration; TGA = Therapeutic Goods Administration (Australia); EMA = European Medicines Agency; NMPA = National Medical Products Administration (China); CDSCO = Central Drugs Standard Control Organisation (India); GLP-1 RA = glucagon-like peptide-1 receptor agonist; GIP = glucose-dependent insulinotropic polypeptide; DPP-4 = dipeptidyl peptidase-4; CKD = chronic kidney disease; CV = cardiovascular; HR = hazard ratio; HbA1c = glycated hemoglobin.\nPrimary endpoints and effect sizes were extracted from pivotal trials and regulatory documentation. For semaglutide (Ozempic®), data are derived from the FLOW trial (NCT03819153), which demonstrated a 24% relative risk reduction in composite kidney and cardiovascular outcomes (Perkovic & Tuttle, 2024; FDA, 2025a). Insulin biosimilar approvals (Merilog® and Kirsty®) were based on comprehensive biosimilarity assessments and switching studies meeting FDA criteria for interchangeability (FDA, 2025b; FDA, 2025c). Approvals of prusogliptin and cofrogliptin in China represent Category 1 innovative drugs developed under expedited pathways (NMPA, 2025a; 2025b). Insulin icodec (Awiqli®) marked the first once-weekly basal insulin, supported by the ONWARDS clinical program (Novo Nordisk, 2025). Tirzepatide (Mounjaro®) and mazdutide approvals highlight the expansion of dual-acting incretin therapies, with tirzepatide being the first dual GIP/GLP-1 agonist in India, and mazdutide the first globally approved dual GLP-1/glucagon receptor agonist (CDSCO, 2025; Innovent Biologics, 2025).\nThe inclusion of sponsor, dosage forms, and regional variations underscores the heterogeneity of global access and the dual trends of innovation (GLP-1 RAs, dual agonists, once-weekly insulins) and affordability (biosimilars). While data presented reflect the most recent regulatory communications and peer-reviewed trial results, post-marketing surveillance and real-world evidence will be essential for confirming long-term efficacy and safety across diverse patient populations.\nInsulin Biosimilars\nA landmark in 2025 diabetes care was the approval of two major insulin biosimilars in the United States, addressing the urgent issue of insulin affordability. For decades, insulin pricing has remained a global controversy, with millions of patients struggling to afford essential therapy. In the United States alone, approximately 8.4 million patients rely on insulin, many of whom experience high out-of-pocket costs (American Diabetes Association [ADA], 2025). Against this backdrop, the FDA’s approval of Merilog® in February 2025 and Kirsty® in July 2025 marked critical advances in patient access.25\nMerilog (Insulin Aspart-szjj): First Rapid-Acting Biosimilar\nOn February 14, 2025, the U.S. FDA approved Merilog® (insulin aspart-szjj), manufactured by Sanofi-Aventis, as the first rapid-acting insulin biosimilar in the U.S. market (FDA, 2025b). Merilog is biosimilar to Novo Nordisk’s NovoLog® (insulin aspart) and is indicated for improving glycemic control in both adults and pediatric patients with diabetes mellitus.26,27 Approval was based on a totality-of-evidence approach, which included extensive analytical, nonclinical, and clinical confirmatory data. The FDA concluded that there were no clinically meaningful differences between Merilog and NovoLog in terms of purity, potency, safety, or efficacy (FDA, 2025b). Importantly, Merilog was launched in both 10 mL multiple-dose vials and 3 mL SoloStar prefilled pens, offering flexibility in clinical practice (Sanofi, 2025).\nKirsty (Insulin Aspart-xjhz): First Interchangeable Rapid-Acting Insulin\nThe FDA expanded this biosimilar landscape further on July 15, 2025, when it approved Kirsty® (insulin aspart-xjhz), developed by Biocon Biologics, as the first interchangeable rapid-acting insulin aspart biosimilar (FDA, 2025c). This designation is highly significant: under U.S. law, an “interchangeable” biosimilar can be substituted at the pharmacy level without prescriber intervention, subject to state pharmacy laws. The designation was supported by results from a randomized phase 3 switching study (NCT03760068) involving 478 patients with type 1 diabetes over 24 weeks, comparing insulin aspart-xjhz to NovoLog®. Findings showed no clinically meaningful differences in efficacy, immunogenicity, or safety between the biosimilar and its reference product (Biocon Biologics, 2025).\nClinical and Market Implications\nThe introduction of Merilog and Kirsty represents a major step forward in affordability. Insulin biosimilars are projected to reduce treatment costs by 21–79% depending on market dynamics, potentially saving billions in healthcare expenditures over the next decade (Research and Markets, 2025). By expanding treatment accessibility, biosimilars are expected to improve adherence and glycemic control, thereby lowering the risk of long-term diabetes complications.In the broader context of 2025, these approvals align with global trends emphasizing sustainability in diabetes care—balancing innovation (e.g., semaglutide renal indication, dual agonists) with affordability through biosimilars (see Table 1). The regulatory milestones of Merilog and Kirsty are visually situated in the 2025 drug approval timeline (Fig. 3).\nThis figure depicts the chronological sequence of landmark regulatory approvals for novel antidiabetic therapies in 2025. Approvals in the United States (FDA, 💊) included semaglutide (Ozempic®) with a chronic kidney disease indication (January 28), Merilog® (insulin aspart-szjj), the first rapid-acting insulin biosimilar (February 14), and Kirsty® (insulin aspart-xjhz), the first interchangeable rapid-acting insulin biosimilar (July 15). In China (NMPA, 🌏), prusogliptin (January 8) and cofrogliptin (February 19) were approved as novel DPP-4 inhibitors, followed by insulin icodec (Awiqli®), the first once-weekly basal insulin (February 19), and mazdutide, the world’s first dual GLP-1/glucagon receptor agonist (June 27). In India (CDSCO, 🇮🇳), tirzepatide (Mounjaro®) received approval on June 26 as the first dual GIP/GLP-1 receptor agonist available in that market. Finally, in Australia (TGA, 🇦🇺), semaglutide’s CKD indication was expanded in August 2025. Together, these regulatory milestones highlight 2025 as a watershed year for diabetes therapeutics, integrating innovations in multi-organ protection, biosimilar affordability, and dual-acting incretin strategies.28\nNovel DPP-4 Inhibitors (China)\nPrusogliptin (Shan Ze Ping, CSPC Ouyi)\nIn January 2025, the National Medical Products Administration (NMPA) approved prusogliptin (善泽平, Shan Ze Ping), developed by CSPC Ouyi, as a Category 1 innovative DPP-4 inhibitor (CSPC Pharmaceutical Group, 2025; NMPA, 2025a). In pivotal phase 3 studies, once-daily prusogliptin 100 mg demonstrated significant HbA1c reduction (− 0.61% vs placebo at 24 weeks) and was non-inferior to sitagliptin 100 mg, with efficacy sustained through 52 weeks (Wang et al., 2024). When added to metformin, prusogliptin achieved a placebo-adjusted HbA1c reduction of − 0.63%, with neutral effects on body weight and minimal hypoglycemia risk (Xu et al., 2022). Pharmacokinetic analyses confirmed stable inhibition of DPP-4 activity, and no dose adjustments were required in patients with mild to moderate renal impairment (Liu et al., 2025).\nCofrogliptin (Bei Chang Ping, Haisco)\nOn February 19, 2025, the NMPA approved cofrogliptin (倍长平, Bei Chang Ping), developed by Haisco Pharmaceutical, as another Category 1 innovative drug (NMPA, 2025b). Cofrogliptin is the world’s first ultra-long-acting oral DPP-4 inhibitor, with a dosing interval of once every two weeks (Q2W). In a large phase 3 non-inferiority trial, cofrogliptin 10 mg or 25 mg Q2W achieved HbA1c reductions of − 0.6% to − 1.0% and was non-inferior to linagliptin 5 mg QD; benefits were maintained during a 28-week open-label extension (Ren et al., 2024; Gao et al., 2025). This extended-interval dosing model, informed by model-informed drug development (MIDD) approaches, reduces pill burden and has the potential to improve long-term adherence in real-world practice (Gao et al., 2024).29\nMechanistic Rationale and Class Effects\nBoth prusogliptin and cofrogliptin exert their effect by inhibiting dipeptidyl peptidase-4 (DPP-4), thereby increasing circulating levels of GLP-1 and GIP. This mechanism enhances glucose-dependent insulin secretion from β-cells, suppresses glucagon secretion from α-cells, reduces hepatic glucose production, and slows gastric emptying (Florentin et al., 2022; Ahrén, 2019). The result is improved glycemic control with weight neutrality and a low intrinsic risk of hypoglycemia, especially when not combined with sulfonylureas or insulin. These mechanistic and clinical features are illustrated in Fig. 4.\nFigure 4. Mechanism of DPP-4 inhibition and clinical outcomes. By preventing incretin degradation, prusogliptin and cofrogliptin enhance glucose-dependent insulin secretion, reduce glucagon, slow gastric emptying, and lower hepatic glucose output, leading to improved HbA1c with weight neutrality and low hypoglycemia risk.30\nComparative Clinical and Regulatory Profiles\nWhile both agents belong to the same class, their clinical positioning diverges. Prusogliptin represents a daily oral DPP-4 inhibitor with a profile similar to existing class members, but with the distinction of being locally innovated in China. In contrast, cofrogliptin’s biweekly dosing represents a global first, targeting adherence and treatment convenience. Their complementary characteristics highlight the dual direction of China’s antidiabetic drug strategy in 2025: incremental clinical innovation and pharmacokinetic breakthroughs. This contrast is summarized in Table 2 and illustrated in Figure 5.\nTable 2. Table 2 compares the two Category 1 innovative DPP-4 inhibitors approved in China in 2025. Prusogliptin (Shan Ze Ping, CSPC) was approved in January 2025 following phase 3 trials demonstrating non-inferiority to sitagliptin and significant HbA1c reduction with low risk of hypoglycemia (Wang et al., 2024; Xu et al., 2022). Cofrogliptin (Bei Chang Ping, Haisco) was approved in February 2025 as the world’s first ultra-long-acting, Q2W DPP-4 inhibitor, confirmed in non-inferiority trials against daily linagliptin with durable glycemic benefits (Ren et al., 2024; Gao et al., 2024, 2025). Both agents maintain the class advantages of weight neutrality and glucose-dependent action, minimizing hypoglycemia risk (Florentin et al., 2022; Ahrén, 2019).\nAdvanced Insulin Formulations\nInsulin Icodec (Awiqli) — A Once-Weekly Basal Insulin\nIn February 2025, the National Medical Products Administration (NMPA) approved insulin icodec injection (Awiqli®, 诺和期), developed by Novo Nordisk, as the world’s first once-weekly basal insulin. Unlike daily basal insulins (e.g., insulin glargine, insulin degludec), insulin icodec is engineered with a C20 fatty diacid side chain that binds strongly but reversibly to serum albumin, forming a circulating depot for sustained, slow release over seven days. This pharmacological profile provides flat, stable insulin levels, reducing variability and supporting effective glycemic control with just one injection per week.31\nClinical Evidence — ONWARDS Phase 3 Program\nThe ONWARDS clinical program, comprising six pivotal trials, evaluated insulin icodec in insulin-naïve and insulin-experienced patients with type 2 diabetes (T2D) as well as individuals with type 1 diabetes (T1D).\n-\nIn insulin-naïve T2D patients, once-weekly icodec achieved HbA1c reductions of ~ 1.3%, non-inferior to daily insulin degludec, with no increase in severe hypoglycemia (ONWARDS 1, 2).\n-\nIn insulin-experienced T2D patients, icodec maintained glycemic control comparable to glargine U100, with high patient satisfaction (ONWARDS 3, 4).\n-\nIn T1D, ONWARDS 6 confirmed non-inferiority but emphasized the need for careful titration and education to minimize hypoglycemia risk.\nCollectively, the ONWARDS data demonstrated that weekly dosing did not compromise safety or efficacy, with treatment satisfaction significantly higher among patients randomized to icodec.\nAdherence and Patient-Centered Benefits\nThe once-weekly dosing reduces the injection burden from 365 to 52 injections per year, directly addressing patient concerns of treatment fatigue, complexity, and injection anxiety. Early patient-reported outcomes highlight greater confidence, less distress, and improved quality of life with icodec compared to daily insulins. This innovation may help overcome therapeutic inertia, a major barrier to timely insulin initiation. Furthermore, by lowering injection frequency, insulin icodec has the potential to improve adherence in low- and middle-income settings, where irregular dosing and supply chain issues often compromise glycemic control.\nClinical and Regulatory Significance\nThe 2025 approval of insulin icodec represents a transformative advancement in insulin therapy. By combining a novel albumin-binding mechanism with proven glycemic efficacy and favorable safety, insulin icodec has the potential to reshape the landscape of basal insulin therapy, simplify patient care, and improve long-term outcomes globally.\nTable 3\nClinical and regulatory profile of insulin icodec (Awiqli®) compared with traditional basal insulins\n|\nAttribute\n|\nInsulin Icodec (Awiqli®, Novo Nordisk)\n|\nDaily Basal Insulins (Glargine, Degludec)\n|\n|\nRegulatory milestone\n|\nApproved by NMPA (China), Feb 2025; protraction via zinc or fatty acid chains → once-daily action\n|\n|\nDosing frequency\n|\nOnce weekly (QW)\n|\nOnce daily (QD)\n|\n|\nONWARDS trial HbA1c effect\n|\nHbA1c reduction ~ 1.3% in T2D (non-inferior to degludec, glargine)\n|\nHbA1c reduction ~ 1.2–1.4% depending on regimen\n|\n|\nHypoglycemia risk\n|\nComparable to daily insulins in T2D; TGA = Therapeutic Goods Administration; QD = once daily; QW = once weekly; T2D = type 2 diabetes; T1D = type 1 diabetes.\nDual and Novel Mechanism Drugs\nTirzepatide: Dual GIP/GLP-1 Receptor Agonist (India, June 2025)\nIn June 2025, the Central Drugs Standard Control Organisation (CDSCO) of India approved tirzepatide (Mounjaro®, Eli Lilly), expanding access to the world’s first dual glucose-dependent insulinotropic polypeptide (GIP) and glucagon-like peptide-1 (GLP-1) receptor agonist in this market (Reuters, 2025; Business Standard, 2025; BioSpectrum India, 2025). The KwikPen formulation was authorized for once-weekly subcutaneous administration across titration doses ranging from 2.5 mg to 15 mg. The efficacy of tirzepatide has been established through the SURPASS phase 3 program. In the pivotal SURPASS J-mono trial, conducted in Japanese patients with type 2 diabetes, tirzepatide demonstrated superior glycemic efficacy and body weight reduction compared with dulaglutide 0.75 mg, which is the standard comparator dose in Japan (Kadowaki et al., 2022). At 52 weeks, tirzepatide 5 mg, 10 mg, and 15 mg achieved mean HbA1c reductions of − 2.37% to − 2.55%, compared with − 1.30% for dulaglutide, alongside dose-dependent weight loss (Kadowaki et al., 2022). Patient-reported outcomes confirmed higher treatment satisfaction with tirzepatide relative to dulaglutide (Terauchi et al., 2022). Furthermore, the recently completed SURPASS-CVOT trial (2025) demonstrated that tirzepatide was non-inferior to dulaglutide for major adverse cardiovascular events (MACE)"
